People v. Masterson


Docket No. 93579


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


207 Ill. 2d 305; 798 N.E.2d 735; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 1425; 278 Ill. Dec. 351



October 2, 2003, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 21, 2003.
Appeal after remand at People v. Masterson, 395 Ill. App. 3d 1116, 986 N.E.2d 806, 2009 Ill. App. LEXIS 5594, 369 Ill. Dec. 493 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the First District; heard in that court on appeal from the Circuit Court of Cook County, the Hon. Colleen McSweeney Moore, Judge, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed; cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant accused was charged with attempted aggravated criminal sexual assault and indecent solicitation of a child. While those charges were pending, appellee State of Illinois instituted a civil commitment proceeding, pursuant to Illinois' Sexually Dangerous Persons Act (SDPA). The trial court ordered commitment, and the Appellate Court of Illinois affirmed the trial court's judgment. The state supreme court granted leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: An accused was charged with attempted aggravated criminal sexual assault and indecent solicitation of a child, and the State instituted a civil commitment proceeding, pursuant to Illinois' SDPA, while the charges were pending. The trial court held a hearing on the State's petition and ordered commitment. The state supreme court held that (1) the term "mental disorder," as used in the SDPA, meant a congenital or acquired condition affecting the emotional or volitional capacity that predisposed a person to commit sex offenses and resulted in serious difficulty controlling sexual behavior; (2) the U.S. Supreme Court's Crane decision required that, before a court find that a person was sexually dangerous and could be committed under the SPDA, the court had to find that it was substantially probable that the person would commit sex offenses in the future, if he or she was not confined; and (3) because neither the U.S. Supreme Court nor the Supreme Court of Illinois had adopted the standard which the U.S. Supreme Court announced in Crane at the time of the accused's commitment hearing, remand was required for a new hearing to determine if the accused should be committed.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the trial court's and intermediate appellate court's judgments and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

State v. Varner


Docket No. 90151


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


207 Ill. 2d 425; 800 N.E.2d 794; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 1424; 279 Ill. Dec. 506



October 2, 2003, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended November 20, 2003.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Varner v. Illinois, 540 U.S. 1225, 158 L. Ed. 2d 164, 124 S. Ct. 1519, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 1739 (2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Varner (In re Varner), 315 Ill. App. 3d 626, 734 N.E.2d 226, 2000 Ill. App. LEXIS 668, 248 Ill. Dec. 518 (Ill. App. Ct. 2d Dist., 2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court of Lake County (Illinois) found defendant to be a sexually violent person under the Sexually Violent Persons Commitment Act, 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 207/1 et seq. (1998). Both the appellate court and the state supreme court affirmed. The United States Supreme Court subsequently granted defendant's petition for writ of certiorari, vacated the judgment of the state supreme court, and remanded the cause for further consideration.


OVERVIEW: On remand, defendant argued that the Crane decision required a specific lack-of-control finding. The state supreme court disagreed, concluding that Crane did not hold that the U.S. Constitution required a specific determination by the fact finder in every case that a person lacked volitional control because Crane upheld the commitment in Hendricks as constitutional, even though there was no specific lack-of-control determination in Hendricks. The Illinois Act conformed to Hendricks and Crane because it required proof of the commission of a prior offense and contained definitions that supplied the constitutionally required elements for civil commitment. A fact finder properly instructed with the necessary definitions did not need to receive additional separate instruction on lack of control. Defendant's jury was properly instructed with definitions of the statutory terms relevant to his case so as to avoid any constitutional impediment. Thus, defendant's commitment did not run afoul of substantive due process.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court, affirming the circuit court's order committing defendant to the custody of the Illinois Department of Human Services for institutional care and treatment in a secure facility, was affirmed.

 

279 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lampitok


Docket No. 93699


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


207 Ill. 2d 231; 798 N.E.2d 91; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 1422; 278 Ill. Dec. 244



September 18, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
People v. Lampitok, 201 Ill. 2d 595, 786 N.E.2d 193, 2002 Ill. LEXIS 1028, 271 Ill. Dec. 935 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was arrested and charged with unlawful possession of a weapon by a felon, unlawful possession of firearm ammunition by a felon, unlawful possession of a hypodermic syringe, unlawful possession of a controlled substance, and armed violence. Defendant filed a motion to quash arrest and suppress evidence. The circuit court granted the motion. The appellate court (Illinois) affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue before the supreme court was whether the search of defendant's motel room in which a probationer subject to a search condition was staying was reasonable under the Fourth Amendment and Ill. Const. art. I, § 6. The supreme court began by concluding that a probation search of the probationer upon no individualized suspicion would be constitutionally unreasonable; thus, the officers were authorized to search defendant's motel room only if they had reasonable suspicion of a probation violation by the probationer. Based on information the officers received from three sources, the officers had a reasonable basis to investigate whether the probationer had changed residences without prior notification to her probation officer in violation of her probation. However, the officers were only authorized to check whether the probationer was in fact in defendant's room. The officers' search between the mattress and box springs, for example, was beyond the permissible scope of the probation search. Thus, the search was constitutionally unreasonable in its scope.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.

 

280 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People v. Graves


Docket No. 94633


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


207 Ill. 2d 478; 800 N.E.2d 790; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 1423; 279 Ill. Dec. 502



September 18, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Appellate Court for the third District; heard in that court on appeal from the Circuit Court of Bureau County, the Hon. Scott Madson, Judge, presiding.
People v. Graves, 332 Ill. App. 3d 685, 773 N.E.2d 1243, 2002 Ill. App. LEXIS 611, 266 Ill. Dec. 173 (Ill. App. Ct., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part; cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State was granted an appeal from an appellate court (Illinois) decision after defendant appealed the sentence that was imposed after he pleaded guilty to unauthorized theft involving a victim over the age of 60. The appellate court ruled that the penalty for unauthorized theft from a victim over age 60 was unconstitutionally disproportionate to the penalty for theft by deception from a victim of age 60 and vacated defendant's sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant misappropriated a check entrusted to him by a 71-year-old man and his 93-year-old mother. He pleaded guilty to unauthorized theft of more than $ 10,000 but not more than $ 100,000. Because of the victims' ages, he was sentenced to an extended term under 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/5-5-3.2(b)(4)(ii), 5/5-8-2 (2000). In the appellate court, defendant argued that his sentence violated the Proportionate Penalties Clause of the Illinois Constitution, Ill. Const. art. I, § 11 (1970) because the penalty for unauthorized theft, seven to 14 years, was greater than the three to seven year penalty for the identical crime of theft by deception from a victim over age 60 found at 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/5-8-1(a)(5) (2000). The appellate court agreed, finding that because the crimes carried identical elements, the difference in sentence was unconstitutional. The court reversed. The elements of the crimes were not identical. One required a showing of unauthorized control over the victim's property while the other required a showing that control over the property was obtained through deception. As the elements were not identical, the differing penalties were constitutionally sound.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that portion of the appellate court decision that ruled that the two penalties were unconstitutionally disproportionate. The court affirmed the remainder of the appellate court decision. The court remanded the case to the circuit court for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

City of Chicago v. Holland


Docket No. 90585


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


206 Ill. 2d 480; 795 N.E.2d 240; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 1404; 276 Ill. Dec. 887



June 19, 2003, Opinion Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] On direct appeal, trial court's judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Auditor General of the State of Illinois appealed directly from a Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) summary judgment declaring that § 3-1 (30 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/3-1 (2000)) of the Illinois Auditing Act, 30 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/1-1 (2000), violated Ill. Const. art. VIII, § 3, and enjoining the Auditor General from proceeding with an audit of airports operated by plaintiff City of Chicago, Illinois.


OVERVIEW: An amendment to 30 Ill. Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/3-1 (2000) specifically directed the Auditor General to conduct an audit of the City's airport operations. The trial court held that such an audit was on its face in excess of the Auditor General's constitutional powers, but the high court held that the statute, as written, did not necessarily exceed the scope of Ill. Const. art. VIII, § 3 powers, so long as the audit was limited to the use of funds appropriated by the State for the City's use in running the airports. The constitutional violation occurred, instead, in the application of the statute to the proposed audit of the airports, since, in point of fact, they received no State funds at all. Although certain federal grants were paid first to the State and then passed on to the airport, they were never funds appropriated by the State within the meaning of the constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment.

People v. William M. (In re William M.)


Docket No. 93760


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


206 Ill. 2d 595; 795 N.E.2d 269; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 1407; 276 Ill. Dec. 916



June 19, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1]
People v. W.M. (In re W.M.), 328 Ill. App. 3d 974, 767 N.E.2d 846, 2002 Ill. App. LEXIS 192, 263 Ill. Dec. 120 (Ill. App. Ct., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed the decision of an appellate court (Illinois), which ruled that respondent juvenile's failure to file an Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 604(d) motion to withdraw a guilty plea in a juvenile delinquency proceeding did not require dismissal of the juvenile's appeal but did require remand of the cause to the trial court for further proceedings under Rule 604(d).


OVERVIEW: The juvenile pleaded guilty to a burglary charge and was adjudicated as a juvenile delinquent and a ward of the court. After committing the juvenile to the Illinois Department of Corrections, the trial court advised the juvenile that he had a right to appeal but that if he appealed, he was required to file a motion to withdraw his guilty plea or a motion to reconsider his sentence. The juvenile appealed but no motion to withdraw the guilty plea or to reconsider the sentence was filed. The State maintained that the requirement of filing a written motion pursuant to Rule 604(d) was jurisdictional and that the juvenile's failure to file a motion required dismissal. The appellate court ruled that failure to file the motion did not require dismissal of the appeal and remanded the case for proceedings pursuant to Rule 604(d). The court affirmed. The failure to file a Rule 604(d) motion did not deprive an appellate court of jurisdiction in either a juvenile or an adult proceeding. But, strict compliance with Rule 604(d) was necessary to preserve issues for appeal. Thus the appellate court properly found it had no discretion and that remand for compliance with the rule was required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment.

People v. Moss


Docket Nos. 91012, 91013, 91044, 91045, 91046, 91047, 91048, 91049, 91050, 91051, 91052, 91328 cons.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


206 Ill. 2d 503; 795 N.E.2d 208; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 1410; 276 Ill. Dec. 855



June 19, 2003, Opinion Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgements of the circuit court affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with crimes subject to sentence enhancement under 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/33A-1 (2000). In each case, defendants filed pretrial motions alleging that the § 5/33A-1 sentencing enhancements were unconstitutional. Judges in the Cook County Circuit Court (Illinois) found that portions of the sentencing enhancements amendment violated the Illinois Constitution. The State appealed, and the cases were consolidated for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged crimes subject to the "15/20/25-to-life" sentence enhancements of § 5/33A-1 (1999 Ill. Laws 404), where possession of a firearm resulted in an additional 15 years, its discharge resulted in an extra 20 years, while discharge resulting in serious injury or death resulted in an enhancement of 25 years to life. The supreme court had ruled that the 15-year enhancement for armed robbery while in possession of a firearm ( 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/18-2(a)(2), (b) (2000)) violated the Proportionate Penalties Clause, Ill. Const. art. I, § 11, and that the attempt statute ( 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/8-4 (2000)), as amended by adding the "15/20/25-to-life" provision to attempted first degree murder, was unconstitutional. It now ruled that as to the statutes for armed robbery ( 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/18-2 (2000)), aggravated kidnapping ( 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/10-2 (2000)), and aggravated vehicular hijacking ( 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/18-4 (2000)), the 15- and 20-year add-ons violated Ill. Const. art. I, § 11. It agreed with one trial judge that the 25-years-to-life add-on to the armed robbery statute did not violate the Proportionate Penalties Clause.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the dismissal of a charge against one defendant of armed robbery with personal discharge of a firearm that proximately caused great bodily harm to the victim. The dismissal of the other charges, as well as trial judges' findings as to the constitutionality vel non of the statutes, was affirmed.

People v. Alexander


Docket No. 93952


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


204 Ill. 2d 472; 791 N.E.2d 506; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 777; 274 Ill. Dec. 414



May 22, 2003, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Alexander v. Illinois, 540 U.S. 983, 124 S. Ct. 471, 157 L. Ed. 2d 374, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 8034 (U.S., Nov. 3, 2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] On direct appeal, trial court's judgment was reversed and case was remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged in the Circuit Court of Lake County (Illinois) with 45 counts of child pornography, under 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/11-20.1 (2001). After pleading guilty to the first 5 counts in exchange for a 7-year sentence, he withdrew his plea and filed a motion to dismiss, which the trial court granted. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant said the statutory sections defining the substantive child pornography offenses he was charged with, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. The supreme court held the definition of "child," for purposes of the child pornography statute, in 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. As 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/11-20.1(a)(1) and (a)(6) (2001) prohibited, inter alia, making and possessing sexually explicit computer depictions of any actual child under 18, they passed strict scrutiny, so defendant could properly be charged with their violation.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was reversed, and the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

People v. Greco


Docket No. 89940


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


204 Ill. 2d 400; 790 N.E.2d 846; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 774; 274 Ill. Dec. 73



May 8, 2003, Opinion Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed in part and reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged in the Circuit Court of Du Page County (Illinois), with, inter alia, aggravated unlawful possession of special mobile equipment, contrary to 625 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/4-103.2(a)(5) (2000). The trial court dismissed these charges, on constitutional grounds, and the State appealed pursuant to Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 302.


OVERVIEW: The State said defendant lacked standing to challenge the permissive inference in 625 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/4-103(a)(1) (2000) that his possession of stolen special mobile equipment could infer his knowledge it was stolen, as the inference applied only to vehicles. The supreme court held the inference applied to special mobile equipment, and defendant was in danger of being subject to the inference and had standing to challenge it. 625 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/4-103.2(b) (2000) was not constitutional as to special mobile equipment because it violated due process by removing the requirement that special mobile equipment be recently stolen for its possession to give rise to an inference that the possessor knew it was stolen. By removing the recency requirement of the inference, the legislature weakened the probability that the inference was correct. It could not be decided if the definition of special mobile equipment in 625 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/1-191 (2000) was unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant, as there was no fact finding, It was not a case involving first amendment freedoms, so a vagueness challenge could only be resolved by application to the facts.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment finding a statutory inference unconstitutional was affirmed, but the trial court's judgment finding the definition of special mobile equipment unconstitutional was reversed, and the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

People v. De La Paz


Docket No. 93208


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


204 Ill. 2d 426; 791 N.E.2d 489; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 775; 274 Ill. Dec. 397



May 8, 2003, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected June 19, 2003.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by De La Paz v. Illinois, 540 U.S. 922, 124 S. Ct. 320, 157 L. Ed. 2d 221, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 7078 (2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. De La Paz, 332 Ill. App. 3d 1135, 2002 Ill. App. LEXIS 1980 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of armed robbery, armed violence, home invasion, and aggravated battery. Defendant was sentenced to a 55-year extended sentenced on the armed robbery and a concurrent five-year sentence for the aggravated battery. The appellate court affirmed. The Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) dismissed defendant's petition for postconviction relief. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that his extended sentence should have been reversed because the trial court did not comply with Apprendi in sentencing him. Defendant also contended that his postconviction counsel was ineffective for failing to raise the issue of mental fitness to participate in postconviction proceedings. The supreme court found defendant's argument to be without merit. Nothing indicated that defendant did not receive that level of assistance to which he was entitled. All that was required for the postconviction matter was that defendant was able to communicate allegations of constitutional violations to counsel.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of defendant's postconviction petition was affirmed.

Hawthorne v. Vill. of Olympia Fields


Docket No. 93462


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


204 Ill. 2d 243; 790 N.E.2d 832; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 767; 274 Ill. Dec. 59



April 17, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Hawthorne v. Vill. of Olympia Fields, 328 Ill. App. 3d 301, 765 N.E.2d 475, 2002 Ill. App. LEXIS 94, 262 Ill. Dec. 338 (Ill. App. Ct., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the appellate court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee homeowner filed an action in the circuit court for declaratory and injunctive relief against appellant village, after the village denied the homeowner's application for a zoning variance. The parties filed cross-motions for partial summary judgment. The circuit court permanently enjoined the village from regulating day-care homes under its zoning ordinance. The Illinois Court of Appeals affirmed the judgment. The village appealed.


OVERVIEW: The homeowner sought to open a day-care home and obtained a license to do the same from the Illinois Department of Children and Family Services (DCFS), all under the Illinois Child Care Act of 1969, 225 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 10/1 et seq. (2000). The village determined that the homeowner could not operate a day-care home, as such conduct was barred by one of the village's zoning ordinances. The supreme court found that the appellate court did not err when it determined that the zoning ordinance completely excluded day-care homes from operating anywhere in the village. Because it was a non-home-rule unit, the village could not adopt ordinances under a general grant of power that infringed upon the spirit of state law or were repugnant to the general policy of the state. Therefore, the village was prohibited from regulating day-care homes under its zoning ordinance in a manner that conflicted in any way with the Child Care Act or the DCFS regulations adopted pursuant to that Act. In addition, the homeowner was entitled to operate at day-care home at her residence pursuant to the terms of her DCFS license.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.

People v. Donoho


Docket No. 92988


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


204 Ill. 2d 159; 788 N.E.2d 707; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 461; 273 Ill. Dec. 116



April 3, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Donoho, 326 Ill. App. 3d 403, 760 N.E.2d 557, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 874, 260 Ill. Dec. 140 (Ill. App. Ct., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Appellate court's judgment was affirmed, in part, and reversed, in part. Trial court's judgment was affirmed, in part, reversed, in part, and cause was remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a jury trial, the Circuit Court for the 12th Judicial Circuit, Will County (Illinois), convicted defendant of one count of criminal sexual assault and four counts of aggravated criminal sexual abuse. Defendant appealed, and the Appellate Court of Illinois reversed his convictions and remanded the case for a new trial. The state supreme court granted the State's petition for leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with criminal sexual assault, in violation of 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/12-13(a)(3) (1998), and aggravated criminal sexual abuse, in violation of 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/12-16(b) (1998), after his step-children told their mother that defendant had touched their genitals and made them touch his penis. At defendant's trial, the State offered evidence that defendant was convicted of taking indecent liberties with a child when he was 18 years old and the trial court admitted defendant's prior conviction as evidence of his propensity to commit sex offenses. After defendant was convicted of all charges, the trial court used his prior conviction in imposing an enhanced sentence pursuant to 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/12-13(b)(4) (1998). The state supreme court held that (1) the trial court properly admitted defendant's prior conviction under 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/115-7.3 (1998), and the intermediate appellate court erred when it held that the trial court erred by admitting the conviction; but (2) the intermediate appellate court's judgment that the trial court erred by using defendant's prior conviction to enhance his sentence was correct.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the intermediate appellate court's judgment reversing defendant's conviction, but affirmed the intermediate appellate court's judgment finding that the trial court improperly used defendant's prior conviction to enhance defendant's sentence, and remanded the case for further proceedings.

 

290 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


Arangold Corp. v. Zehnder


Docket No. 93836


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


204 Ill. 2d 142; 787 N.E.2d 786; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 460; 272 Ill. Dec. 600



March 20, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Arangold Corp. v. Zehnder, 329 Ill. App. 3d 781, 768 N.E.2d 391, 2002 Ill. App. LEXIS 266, 263 Ill. Dec. 631 (Ill. App. Ct., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tobacco corporation challenged the constitutionality of the Tobacco Products Tax Act of 1995, 35 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 143/10-10 et seq. (West 2000). The Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) and the Court of Appeals upheld the Act against challenges under the Illinois Due Process Clause and the Uniformity Clause. Summary judgment was granted to defendants, the director of revenue and others. Leave to appeal was granted.


OVERVIEW: The corporation argued that the tax imposed by the Act was arbitrary and unreasonable because it selectively targeted for taxation distributors of cigars and chewing tobacco to fund a general welfare program of the state, designed to benefit a broad range of taxpayers. The state's highest court held that the trial court did not err in granting summary judgment to defendants on the due process claim. The corporation presented no compelling reason for construing the Illinois Due Process Clause independently of its federal counterpart. Further, the tax imposed under the Act bore a reasonable relationship to the objective of the Act and it did not violate the Uniformity Clause. As defendants asserted a justification for the tax, that the Act taxed tobacco distributors because their products caused diseases that conceivably required long-term care for those persons who could not pay for it, it was then the corporation's burden to come forward with evidence showing that the asserted justification was unsupported by the facts, which the corporation failed to do.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.

People ex rel. Sherman v. Cryns


Docket No. 93412


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


203 Ill. 2d 264; 786 N.E.2d 139; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 450; 271 Ill. Dec. 881



February 21, 2003, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Cryns v. Ill., ex rel. Hewson, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 5592 (U.S., Oct. 6, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
People ex rel. Sherman v. Cryns, 327 Ill. App. 3d 753, 763 N.E.2d 904, 2002 Ill. App. LEXIS 61 (Ill. App. Ct., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant lay midwife sought review of the judgment of the Illinois Appellate Court which reversed the trial court and found appellee Director of the Illinois Department of Professional Regulation had established a prima facie case that the lay midwife violated provisions of the Nursing and Advanced Practice Nursing Act when she participated in a birth, in which the child died, without a license.


OVERVIEW: The child's parents hired the lay midwife to give them advice and assist them in a home delivery. The mother had several prenatal visits. While the midwife was assisting with the delivery, the baby was born in a breech position. The midwife waited before calling paramedics. The baby died. The supreme court held the Director proffered no testimony that a videotape that was actually admitted into evidence was a true and accurate depiction of what it purported to show. Thus, the trial court erred in admitting the videotape. However, the conduct of the midwife during the birth constituted prima facie evidence of "professional nursing" within the meaning of the Act. Throughout her interaction with the parents the midwife was assessing the "healthcare needs" of both the mother and the baby, and making "nursing evaluations" in violation of 225 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 65/5-10(l) (West 2000). The provisions of the Act were not unconstitutionally vague as applied to her. Her argument regarding separation of powers was also without merit.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

292 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


Friends of the Parks v. Chi. Park Dist.


Docket No. 93852


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


203 Ill. 2d 312; 786 N.E.2d 161; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 451; 271 Ill. Dec. 903



February 21, 2003, Opinion Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs parks groups and members, sued defendants, a city park district, a state sports facilities authority, a national football club, a stadium partnership, and a city seeking a declaratory judgment that 70 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 3205/3 (West 2000) violated Ill. Const. art. VIII, § 1(a), art. IV, § 8(d). The Illinois trial court granted defendants' motion for summary judgment. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court had to defer to the legislative findings in the Illinois Sports Facilities Authority Act, 70 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 3205/1 et seq. (West 2000), as amended by 1999 Ill. Laws 935, unless there was a threshold showing that the findings were evasive and that the Act's purpose was principally to benefit private interests. In view of the stadium's long history of hosting events that had incidentally benefitted private interests, the court could discern no evasive or deceptive purpose in the legislative findings. Because an adjacent park was not dedicated to a particular use or purpose, the legislature could determine its use. The park and the stadium were public facilities already owned and operated by the park district. No private property interests were at issue. There was no abdication of control of the property to the football club. The public would enjoy a fully renovated, multi-use stadium, instead of a deteriorating 78-year-old facility. Those results did not violate the public trust doctrine even though the club would also benefit. Because 1999 Ill. Laws 935 was certified, under the enrolled-bill doctrine, judicial review under Ill. Const. art. IV, § 8(d) was precluded.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

293 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People v. J.W. (In re J.W.)


Docket No. 92116


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


204 Ill. 2d 50; 787 N.E.2d 747; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 454; 272 Ill. Dec. 561



February 21, 2003, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected March 1, 2003. As Corrected March 26, 2003.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by J.W. v. Ill., 2003 U.S. LEXIS 6980 (U.S., Oct. 6, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: In re J.W., 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 1310 (Ill. App. Ct., July 9, 2001)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, vacated in part and remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After admitting to two counts of aggravated criminal sexual assault, defendant 12-year-old appealed the Illinois appellate court's affirming of probation requirements that he register under the Sex Offender Registration Act, 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 150/1 et seq. (West 2000), for the rest of his life, arguing substantive due process, double jeopardy, and cruel and unusual punishment, and challenged being banished from his home town.


OVERVIEW: Because defendant challenged the constitutionality of the probation conditions, a failure to comply with Ill Sup. Ct. R. 604(d) did not bar the appeal. Under 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 150/2(A-5) (West 2000), defendant was a juvenile sex offender due to the aggravated criminal sexual assault adjudication, and qualified as a sexual predator under 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 150/2(E)(1) (West 2000). The Act was not punitive and therefore did not violate the Eighth Amendment, because the dissemination of information under the Act was restricted as to juveniles; the double jeopardy argument likewise failed. 705 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 405/5-715(2)(r) (West 2000) allowed restricting a minor defendant's ability to be in a designated area as a condition of probation, if the court included terms for entering the area for legitimate purposes. Due to the nature of the offenses and hostile public sentiment, removing defendant from the location where the offenses took place was in his best interest, and was a benefit to his and his victims' rehabilitation. But, the trial court had not determined what appropriate terms for entering the geographic area should be applied and thus was overly broad.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court, affirming the judgment of the trial court, was affirmed in part, vacated in part, and remanded. That portion of the trial court's order imposing the banishment condition was vacated, and upon remand, the court could consider whether the geographic travel restriction was still warranted and, if so, what appropriate terms for entering the geographic area would be applied.

Wakulich v. Mraz


Docket No. 92128


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


203 Ill. 2d 223; 785 N.E.2d 843; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 19; 271 Ill. Dec. 649



February 6, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wakulich v. Mraz, 322 Ill. App. 3d 768, 255 Ill. Dec. 907, 751 N.E.2d 1, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 215 (1st Dist. 2001).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mother filed an action individually and as special administrator of her daughter's estate against defendants, two brothers and their father, alleging negligence. The Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) dismissed the action, and the mother appealed. The Appellate Court of Illinois affirmed the trial court's judgment in part and reversed it in part, and the state supreme court granted the mother's petition for leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: A mother sued two brothers and their father, pursuant to the Illinois Wrongful Death Act, 740 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 180/0.01 et seq. (West 1996), and the Illinois Survival Act, 755 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/27-6 (West 1996), after her 16-year-old daughter consumed alcohol at the father's home, and died. The mother claimed that the brothers encouraged her daughter to drink, that they assumed a duty of caring for her daughter after she became unconscious, and that her daughter died because the brothers acted negligently. The state supreme court held that (1) Illinois did not recognize a common law cause of action for injuries arising out of the sale or gift of alcohol and the trial court ruled correctly that the father could not be held liable under the common law or under Illinois' Dramshop Act, 235 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/6-21 (West 2000), because the brothers provided the daughter with alcohol while she was at the father's home; but (2) the mother's complaint stated a cause of action against the brothers for negligence based on breach of a voluntary undertaking to care for her daughter, and the appellate court properly reversed the trial court's judgment dismissing that claim.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's judgment affirming in part and reversing in part the trial court's judgment dismissing the mother's complaint.

People v. Thurow


Docket No. 90911


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


203 Ill. 2d 352; 786 N.E.2d 1019; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 20; 272 Ill. Dec. 185



February 6, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
 People v. Thurow, 318 Ill. App. 3d 128, 252 Ill. Dec. 342, 742 N.E.2d 880, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 2 (3d Dist. 2001).



DISPOSITION: Appellate court judgement affirmed in part and reversed in part; circuit court judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury sitting in the Circuit Court of the 12th Judicial Circuit, Will County (Illinois), found defendant guilty of simple involuntary manslaughter, and the trial court imposed an enhanced sentence of eight years in prison. On appeal, the Appellate Court of Illinois vacated defendant's sentence and ordered the trial court to impose a sentence no longer than five years. The state supreme court granted the State's petition for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with involuntary manslaughter of a family or household member, in violation of 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/9-3(f) (West 1998), after she admitted placing her hand over the mouth of an infant when he would not stop crying, and a forensic pathologist opined that the child died from asphyxiation. Defendant lived in the same household as the child's mother and defendant and the child's mother shared responsibility for taking care of each other's children. At the end of defendant's trial, the trial court instructed the jury on the elements of involuntary manslaughter, but did not ask the jury to find that the victim was a member of defendant's family or household. After the jury found defendant guilty of involuntary manslaughter, the trial court determined that defendant was eligible for an enhanced sentence because the victim was a member of defendant's household and was also a person of tender years, and it imposed an enhanced sentence. The state supreme court held that the trial court violated the United States Supreme Court's Apprendi decision by not submitting the question of the victim's status to the jury, but the error was harmless.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's judgment that defendant's sentence was imposed in violation of the United States Supreme Court's Apprendi decision, but because the error was harmless, the supreme court reversed the intermediate appellate court's judgment vacating defendant's sentence, and it affirmed the eight-year sentence imposed by the trial court.

People v. Pomykala


Docket No. 93089-Agenda 21-September 2002.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


203 Ill. 2d 198; 784 N.E.2d 784; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 7; 271 Ill. Dec. 230



January 24, 2003, Decided  
January 24, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
 People v. Pomykala, 326 Ill. App. 3d 390, 259 Ill. Dec. 855, 759 N.E.2d 916, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 784 (2001).



DISPOSITION: Appellate court's judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of Illinois sought review from an order of the appellate court (Illinois), which reversed defendant's conviction for reckless homicide and remanded the matter for a new trial, based on a finding that § 9-3(b) of the Criminal Code of 1961, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/9-3(b) (West 2000), created an unconstitutional mandatory presumption of recklessness.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of reckless homicide, which occurred when the car he was driving crossed the median and struck an oncoming car, killing a person therein. The evidence showed that defendant knew his car brakes were not working properly and that defendant was intoxicated. Defendant objected to the trial court's jury instruction that was based on § 9-3(b), but the trial court overruled his objection and he was convicted. On appeal, the appellate court found that § 9-3(b) created a mandatory presumption that violated defendant's due process rights in that the presumption shifted the burden to defendant to show that he was not acting recklessly. On appeal, the court agreed with the appellate court. The court found that the presumption was not a permissive one because upon a finding that defendant was intoxicated, the jury was to presume that he was reckless unless he proved that he was not. This burden-shifting was unconstitutional. However, the court found that § 9-3(b) was severable from the remainder of the statute. The court noted that the jury instruction was not a harmless error because the result might have been different without it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision.

People v. Morgan


Docket No. 90891


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


203 Ill. 2d 470; 786 N.E.2d 994; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 1; 272 Ill. Dec. 160



January 24, 2003, Opinion Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court of Sangamon County (Illinois) effectively dismissed the charge of attempted first degree murder against defendant by ruling that the attempt statute, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/8-4 (West 2000), amended by 1991 Ill. Laws 404, § 5 (effective January 1, 2000), (amended statute) was unconstitutional. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court found the amended statute was unconstitutional since the "15-20-25 to life"-mandated penalties for attempted first degree murder (the attempt) violated the proportionate penalties clause of Ill. Const. art. I, § 11 (1970) as it permitted a person who failed to kill the victim and was convicted of the attempt to be sentenced to a much greater term than the person who actually killed his victim. The amended statute provided that a person who intended to kill but failed to kill was to be convicted of the attempt, whether or not mitigating circumstances existed, and was to be sentenced to six to 30 years, with the mandatory addition of 15, 20, or 25 years to life, depending on whether a firearm was in the person's possession, discharged, or the cause of bodily harm or the death of another. A person who intended to kill and killed, whether or not a firearm was used, was to be subject to a lesser 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/5-8-1(1.5) (West 2000) sentencing range and was even eligible for 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/5-6-1 (West 2000) probation if the person demonstrated mitigators he acted under serious provocation or possessed unreasonable belief that self-defense was necessary.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment.

People v. Gherna


Docket No. 92554


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


203 Ill. 2d 165; 784 N.E.2d 799; 2003 Ill. LEXIS 2; 271 Ill. Dec. 245



January 24, 2003, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
 People v. Gherna, 325 Ill. App. 3d 157, 258 Ill. Dec. 486, 756 N.E.2d 468, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 745 (4th Dist. 2001).



DISPOSITION: Appellate court judgment reversed; circuit court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court granted defendant's suppression motion, rejecting the State's contention that the officers' encounter with defendant, who was driving the vehicle searched, was entirely consensual. The Appellate Court of Illinois, First District, First Division, reversed the trial court decision. Defendant appealed under Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 604(a).


OVERVIEW: The supreme court allowed the appeal under Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 315. Bicycle officers first saw two women in a vehicle. The officers then saw a beer container in the vehicle, suspected underage drinking, and questioned defendant, whose daughter was the passenger. When the officers confirmed the container was not open, the trial court factually determined their reason for detaining for underage drinking ended. However, the officers then saw a credit card, asked defendant to exit the vehicle to talk to her and conduct a search, saw a baggie of cocaine fall out of her pocket, arrested her, and charged her with possession of a controlled substance. Defendant remained seized, unreasonably, after officers saw the container was never opened, within the meaning of the Fourth Amendment and Ill. Const. art. I, § 6 (1970), because a reasonable innocent person in defendant's position would not believe that she was free to disregard the police (based upon their show of authority), and go about her business. The continued detention, not supported by any reasonable, articulable suspicion of criminal activity, was an unconstitutional unreasonable seizure tainting her subsequent consent to search.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the appeals court's judgment and affirmed the trial court's judgment.

People v. Johnson

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Montgomery County Circuit Court (Illinois) denied petitioner convicted murderer's post-conviction petition as to five claims which had survived a motion to dismiss and two claims which had survived a motion for summary judgment after he pled guilty to murder and two aggravated criminal sexual assaults, he was sentenced to death, and the convictions and sentence were affirmed on direct appeal. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner pled guilty to a murder and two aggravated criminal sexual assaults. He was sentenced to death. His convictions and sentence were affirmed on direct appeal. He then petitioned under the Post-Conviction Hearing Act, 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/122-1 et seq. (West 1994), and alleged 16 original claims and two supplemental claims. It first applied the manifest error standard to find no error occurred in finding he was fit to plead guilty and that his trial counsel did not render ineffective assistance. It then applied the de novo standard to find meritless his claims that appointed public defenders had an inherent conflict of interest, that his attorneys were ineffective for not arguing that similarly-situated defendants received lesser sentences, and that the death penalty statute was unconstitutional.

People v. Carrera


Docket No. 91465


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


203 Ill. 2d 1; 783 N.E.2d 15; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 1644; 270 Ill. Dec. 440



December 19, 2002, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
 People v. Carrera, 321 Ill. App. 3d 582, 254 Ill. Dec. 934, 748 N.E.2d 652, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 194 (1st Dist. 2001).



DISPOSITION: Appellate court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted the State's petition for leave to appeal from an order of the Appellate Court of Illinois, First District, Third Division, which reversed a circuit court's denial of defendant's second motion to quash arrest and suppress evidence and remanded for further proceedings. Defendant was convicted of three counts of possession of a controlled substance with intent to deliver and was sentenced to concurrent terms of 15 years in prison.


OVERVIEW: The State acknowledged that police made an extraterritorial arrest pursuant to an amendment to Ill. Comp. Stat. § 5/7-4-8, which was later held to be unconstitutional. The state argued that, as of the date of defendant's arrest, the amendment to § 5/7-4-8 had not been declared unconstitutional. Thus, the good-faith exception to the exclusionary rule applied because police did not violate defendant's substantive constitutional rights in effecting the arrest. The court acknowledged that the exclusionary rule applied where police made an extraterritorial arrest without appropriate statutory authority. However, applying the good-faith exception after 1989 Ill. Laws 404 was declared facially unconstitutional, ran counter to the court's single subject clause and void ab initio jurisprudence. Giving effect to the historical fact that the amendment existed at the time of defendant's arrest effectively resurrected the amendment and provided a grace period during which Illinois citizens were subjected to extraterritorial arrests without proper authorization. The court concluded that police effectuated an unlawful arrest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.

During post-conviction proceedings, defendant filed a pro se motion for mitochondrial DNA testing pursuant to 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/116-3 (West 2000). The State appealed. After considering that appeal, the state supreme court found that the State's appeal had to be dismissed. It so concluded because it found that although the granting of the motion could be characterized as a final judgment, the State had not shown any authority for its proposition that it had the right to appeal the granting of the motion.


OUTCOME: The appeal was dismissed.

People v. Cox


Docket No. 90759


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


202 Ill. 2d 462; 782 N.E.2d 275; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 961; 270 Ill. Dec. 81



December 5, 2002, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] People v. Cox, 318 Ill. App. 3d 161, 251 Ill. Dec. 133, 739 N.E.2d 1066, 2000 Ill. App. LEXIS 954 (5th Dist. 2000).
People v. Cox, 318 Ill. App. 3d 161, 739 N.E.2d 1066, 2000 Ill. App. LEXIS 954 (Ill. App. Ct. 5th Dist., 2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with unlawful possession of cannabis, in violation of 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 550/4(a) (West 1998), and she filed a motion to suppress evidence. The Circuit Court of Wayne County (Illinois) granted the motion, and the Appellate Court of Illinois, Fifth District, affirmed. The state supreme court granted the State's petition for leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: A police officer stopped defendant's vehicle because it did not have a rear registration light. Although the officer did not smell cannabis in defendant's vehicle or have any reason to request the assistance of other officers, he called a deputy and asked him to bring his dog to the scene. The deputy arrived at the scene 15 minutes after defendant was stopped and walked his dog around defendant's car. When the dog alerted, the officers searched the car and defendant and found traces of marijuana in the car and a small amount of marijuana on defendant. The state supreme court held that (1) the officer who stopped defendant did not have specific and articulable facts justifying his request that the deputy bring his dog to the scene of the traffic stop or that the deputy walk his dog around defendant's car; (2) defendant's detention, considered in light of the scope and purpose of the traffic stop, was overly long; and (3) under the circumstances, the trial court properly suppressed the evidence recovered by police.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's judgment.

People ex rel. Dep't of Prof'l Regulation v. Manos


Docket No. 93299


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


202 Ill. 2d 563; 782 N.E.2d 237; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 966; 270 Ill. Dec. 43



December 5, 2002, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
 People v. Manos, 326 Ill. App. 3d 698, 260 Ill. Dec. 364, 761 N.E.2d 208, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 883 (2001).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Illinois Department of Professional Regulation sued defendant dentists, seeking an order requiring the dentists to produce patient dental records and appointment schedules named in a subpoena duces tecum. The Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) ordered the dentists to produce the records, but the Appellate Court of Illinois reversed part of that order. The state supreme court granted the Department's petition for leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: The Illinois Department of Professional Regulation sued two dentists after they refused to produce patient dental records and appointment schedules named in a subpoena duces tecum. The trial court ordered the dentists to produce the records and appointment schedules but, on appeal, the intermediate appellate court held that the dental records were protected by the physician-patient privilege statute, 735 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/8-802 (West 2000), and that the Department could not compel the dentists to produce those records. The state supreme court held that (1) the dentists were surgeons for purposes of 735 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/8-802 (West 1994), and they could claim the physician-patient privilege; (2) investigations conducted by the Department were not listed as an exception to the physician-patient privilege under 735 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/8-802 (West 1994), and the Department could not compel the dentists to provide confidential information on their patients; and (3) the Department could not compel the dentists to release sanitized versions of their patient records because releasing sanitized versions would violate the physician-patient privilege.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's judgment.

People v. Law


Docket No. 93389


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


202 Ill. 2d 578; 782 N.E.2d 247; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 969; 270 Ill. Dec. 53



December 5, 2002, Opinion Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Circuit court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with violating § 6-16(c) of the Illinois Liquor Control Act of 1934, codified at 235 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/6-16(c) (West 2000). Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the case and have the statute declared unconstitutional. The Circuit Court of Whiteside County (Illinois) granted the motion. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The question before the supreme court was whether 235 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/6-16(c) (West 2000) was unconstitutionally vague. The supreme court stated that in determining whether §6-16(c) was unconstitutionally vague, the proper inquiry was whether the statute gave fair warning as to what conduct was required of an occupant to prevent an intoxicated minor from departing. In upholding the determination that the statute was unconstitutional, the supreme court noted that § 6-16(c) provided no guidance as to the steps that were to be taken to prevent an intoxicated minor from leaving a gathering. In addition, the supreme court noted that a residential occupant who physically detained an intoxicated minor could be criminally culpable for unlawful restraint. Thus posing a difficult choice for the occupant: either comply with the statute and commit a felony, or do nothing and commit a misdemeanor. The supreme court held that § 6-16(c) failed to provide the kind of notice that would enable an ordinary person to understand what they must do to avoid prosecution under the statute.


OUTCOME: The circuit court's dismissal of the action and declaration that the statute at issue was unconstitutional were affirmed.

 

305 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People v. Miller


Docket No. 89795


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


202 Ill. 2d 328; 781 N.E.2d 300; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 950; 269 Ill. Dec. 503



November 21, 2002, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Counsel Corrected December 31, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a 15-year-old juvenile, was convicted of 2 counts of first-degree murder based upon accountability. The Cook County Circuit Court (Illinois) declined to impose the statutorily mandated sentence, holding that application of the statute to defendant would offend the proportionate penalties clause of Ill. Const. art. I, § 11. Instead, the trial court sentenced defendant to 50 years in prison. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was told to be a lookout. Two other individuals then shot two victims. Under the automatic transfer statute, defendant was considered to be an adult for purposes of trial. Under the accountability statute, he was considered equal to the actual shooter. Thus, he was tried as if he were the adult shooter. Under the sentencing statute, he was then subject to the most severe punishment. The trial court ruled that application of the multiple-murder sentencing statute, 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/5-8-1(a)(1)(c)(ii) was highly unconscionable. The supreme court held its holding in Truitt with respect to Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 603 and the State's ability to directly appeal a circuit court's finding of unconstitutionality was overly broad. It noted that, when the three statutes that were applied converged, a court never considered the actual facts of the crime. Thus, it held the penalty mandated by 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/5-8-1(a)(1)(c)(ii), as applied to defendant, was particularly harsh and unconstitutionally disproportionate. A mandatory sentence of natural life in prison with no possibility of parole shocked the moral sense of the community and violated Ill. Const. art. I, § 11.


OUTCOME: The supreme court found the multiple-murder sentencing statute, as applied to defendant, violated the proportionate penalties clause of the Illinois Constitution. It affirmed the trial court's imposition of a term of 50 years' imprisonment.

Unzicker v. Kraft Food Ingredients Corp.


Docket No. 92838


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


203 Ill. 2d 64; 783 N.E.2d 1024; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 957; 270 Ill. Dec. 724



November 21, 2002, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied February 3, 2003, Reported at: 2003 Ill. LEXIS 22.


PRIOR HISTORY:  Unzicker v. Kraft Food Ingredients Corp., 325 Ill. App. 3d 587, 259 Ill. Dec. 351, 758 N.E.2d 474, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 816 (App. Ct. 2001).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sued appellee company for negligence. The company sought contribution from the employee's employer. The jury awarded the employee damages and found the company 1 percent and the employer 99 percent at fault. The trial court applied 735 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/2-1117 (West 1994) and reduced the amount of the employee's recovery. The Illinois Court of Appeals for the Fourth District affirmed. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee was injured while he was installing stainless steel piping at the company's plant. The employee applied for and received workers' compensation benefits. The trial court's application of 735 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/2-1117 and the Illinois Joint Tortfeasor Contribution Act, 740 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 100/0.01 et seq. (West 2000), rendered the verdict somewhat of a loss for the employee, because the company was severally liable for only one percent of the nonmedical damages, and the employer was liable only for contribution in an amount equal to its workers' compensation liability. The employee contended that his recovery should not have been reduced by the application of the statute, as the employer should not have been considered in the allocation of fault. The employee also contended that the statute was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court found that the appellate court properly determined that the employer could be considered in the division of fault under the statute. Furthermore, the employee failed to demonstrate that the statute was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

People v. Swift


Docket No. 91840


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


202 Ill. 2d 378; 781 N.E.2d 292; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 954; 269 Ill. Dec. 495



November 21, 2002, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Illinois v. Swift, 538 U.S. 979, 123 S. Ct. 1803, 155 L. Ed. 2d 669, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 2983 (2003)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at People v. Swift, 2016 IL App (2d) 131052-U, 2016 Ill. App. Unpub. LEXIS 200 (2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Swift, 322 Ill. App. 3d 127, 750 N.E.2d 294, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 388, 255 Ill. Dec. 713 (Ill. App. Ct. 2d Dist., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and vacated in part: sentence vacated and cause remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Illinois appellate court affirmed the trial court's first-degree murder conviction of defendant following a jury's verdict finding him guilty, but vacated his extended-term sentence after it found the statute authorizing the extended-term sentence, 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/5-8-2 (West 1998), was facially unconstitutional. Defendant appealed as a matter of right.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and the victim had a drug-related altercation. Defendant stabbed the victim 21 times. The victim died. Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder. While the basic sentence for first-degree murder was between 20 and 60 years in prison, the State requested that the trial court impose an extended-term sentence. Imposition of an extended-term sentence required that additional facts be proven. The trial court found that the offense was exceptionally brutal and heinous. It then imposed an extended-term sentence of 80 years in prison based on that finding and pursuant to 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/5-8-2(a) (West 1998). The appellate court affirmed the conviction, but vacated the extended-term sentence upon finding 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/5-8-2 to be facially unconstitutional. After defendant appealed, the state supreme court found that the sentence could not stand because it violated Apprendi. It so concluded because it found defendant's sentence could not exceed the 60-year basic first-degree murder sentence based on a factual finding made by the trial court. However, it also found that 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/5-8-2 was not facially unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment that the sentence could not stand was affirmed. However, its conclusion that the sentencing statute was facially unconstitutional was vacated. The conviction entered by the trial court was affirmed, but the sentence was vacated and the case was remanded to the trial court for resentencing.

 

308 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People v. Caballero


Docket No. 88784


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


206 Ill. 2d 65; 794 N.E.2d 251; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 948; 276 Ill. Dec. 356



October 18, 2002, Opinion Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Cook County Circuit Court (Illinois), upon remand, in defendant's second post-conviction petition, held a hearing with respect to the proportionality of defendant's death sentence as compared to the sentences given to three of his co-participants in the murder of three rival gang members, and rejected his petition. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was an active and willing participant in all three killings; he beat one victim, took part in setting up the murders, encouraged the use of a knife instead of a gun, guarded and escorted two victims to their deaths, and knifed the last victim to death. One other co-participant testified for the State and received a 40-year sentence, a second received the death sentence, and the third received 3 consecutive life sentences. Defendant argued primarily that his sentence was disproportionate to the third co-participant. The State was estopped from arguing that defendant's culpability, or rehabilitation potential, was worse than the third co-participant's since it had previously argued the third co-participant's was worse. With respect to the comparative proportionality review for actors in the same crime, the high court analyzed, de novo, the rehabilitative potential, criminal history, and relative culpability factors, to conclude defendant was no less culpable than the third co-participant. Defendant's sentence was not arbitrarily or unreasonably disparate; the two separate juries could reasonably reach different conclusions about the imposition of the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

309 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People v. Purcell


Docket No. 92739


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


201 Ill. 2d 542; 778 N.E.2d 695; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 938; 268 Ill. Dec. 429



October 3, 2002, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at People v. Purcell, 2006 Ill. App. LEXIS 269 (Ill. App. Ct. 2d Dist., Mar. 31, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1]
People v. Purcell, 325 Ill. App. 3d 551, 758 N.E.2d 895, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 837, 259 Ill. Dec. 487 (Ill. App. Ct. 2d Dist., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted in the circuit court of Winnebago County (Illinois) on four counts of first degree murder under 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/9-1(a)(2), (a)(3), (b)(19) (West 2000). On appeal following bail denial, the appellate court declared 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/110-4(b) (West 2000) unconstitutional and remanded the case to the trial court to consider defendant's bail request. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had requested bail and sought a determination that 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/110-4(b) (West 2000) was unconstitutional. This court agreed with the lower court finding that 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/110-4(b) (West 2000) was unconstitutional and that defendant's pretrial bail proceedings deprived him of his constitutional right to due process, but the unconstitutionality of § 110-4(b) was not fatal to the remainder of § 110-4. As Ill. Const. art. I, § 9 expressly protected the right of a defendant to bail unless certain circumstances existed, the prosecution had to have the burden of showing sufficient evidence that a defendant should be denied that constitutional right. As a practical matter, the State was in a better position to present such evidence during a bail hearing. The accused was often in a very poor position to present evidence, being incarcerated and facing fresh criminal charges. Placing the burden on the accused was forcing him to prove a negative. This holding did not serve to invalidate the entire statute. The removal of § 110-4(b) did not undermine the completeness of, nor the ability to execute, the remaining subsections of § 110-4.


OUTCOME: The lower court decision was affirmed.

People v. Nancy F. (In re D.F.)

SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS

DISPOSITION: Appellate court judgment affirmed in part, vacated in part, and reversed in part; circuit court judgment affirmed.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State filed a petition to terminate respondent mother's parental rights pursuant to the Illinois Adoption Act, 750 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 50/1 et seq. (West 1998). People v. Leola B. (In re Curtis B.)


Docket No. 92010


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


203 Ill. 2d 53; 784 N.E.2d 219; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 624; 271 Ill. Dec. 1



September 19, 2002, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As modified upon denial of rehearing, February 3, 2003.
On remand at, Decision reached on appeal by In re C.B., 335 Ill. App. 3d 1180, 2002 Ill. App. LEXIS 5377 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: In re Curtis B., 325 Ill. App. 3d 393, 758 N.E.2d 312, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 613, 259 Ill. Dec. 189 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Court of appeals' judgment was reversed and cause was remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of a judgment of the appellate court (Illinois), granting the motion to dismiss filed by appellee State and finding that a permanency order was not a final order and that 705 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 405/2-28 (1998) violated Ill. Const. art. II, § 1.


OVERVIEW: The trial court conducted a permanency hearing and found that the mother was unable to take care of the child. The mother appealed the trial court's permanency order. The appeal was authorized by 705 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 405/2-28(3) (1998). The appellate court found that § 2-28(3) encroached upon the inherent powers of the judiciary. The supreme court agreed. Despite the language in 705 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 405/2-28(2) (1998), a permanency order was not final. Thus, it was not appealable under Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 304(b)(1). The legislature's attempt to make a nonfinal judgment appealable violated Ill. Const. art. VI, § 6. Accordingly, the appeal provision of 705 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 405/2-28(3), which attempted to make a nonfinal order appealable, was unconstitutional. The supreme court found that appeals from permanency hearings were covered under Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 306(a)(5), which allowed appellant to petition for leave to appeal.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded with directions.

Lyons v. Ryan


Docket No. 92503


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


201 Ill. 2d 529; 780 N.E.2d 1098; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 625; 269 Ill. Dec. 374



September 19, 2002, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1]
 Lyons v. Ryan, 324 Ill. App. 3d 1094, 258 Ill. Dec. 414, 756 N.E.2d 396, (1st Dist. 2001).



DISPOSITION: Court of appeal's judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs filed a suit on behalf of the State of Illinois against defendants, seeking to impose constructive trusts on funds and benefits alleged to have been illegally received by defendants. The Illinois trial court dismissed plaintiffs' suit, finding that plaintiffs lacked standing. The appellate court affirmed the trial court's order. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs argued that they had standing to bring a taxpayer action against defendants on behalf of the State. The supreme court held that it was the State that was actually and substantially interested in the action, and only the Sate could be entitled to the benefits of a successful action. Thus, only the attorney general was empowered to represent the State in litigation when it was the real party in interest. Plaintiffs further argued that they had standing to sue on behalf of the State under 735 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/20-104(b) (West 1998). The supreme court held that the legislature had improperly reduced the attorney general's common law authority by enacting the statute to confer standing upon private citizens to commence and prosecute actions on behalf of the State. Since the statute usurped the powers of the attorney general, it was invalid. The supreme court determined that the statute was unconstitutional to the extent that it purported to confer standing on private citizens to sue in cases where the State was the real party in interest.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.

Stroger v. Reg'l Transp. Auth.


Docket No. 92473


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


201 Ill. 2d 508; 778 N.E.2d 683; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 626; 268 Ill. Dec. 417



September 19, 2002, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1]
Court of appeals' judgment: Stroger v. Reg'l Transp. Auth., 324 Ill. App. 3d 970, 258 Ill. Dec. 346, 756 N.E.2d 328, (1st Dist. 2001).



DISPOSITION: Court of appeals' judgment was reversed and trial court's judgment was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the President of Cook County, Illinois, Board of Commissioners and a Board district representative, sued defendants, the Regional Transportation Authority (RTA), its commuter rail division, and its suburban bus division, for declaratory and injunctive relief involving the method for appointing defendants' board members. The Appeals Court (Illinois) remanded the granting of defendants' motion to dismiss. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: The RTA was established by the Regional Transportation Authority Act (Act), 70 Ill. Comp. Stat. 3615/1.01 et seq. (1998). The supreme court, in reversing the judgment, determined that the Act did not create an additional unit of government in the seven commissioners empowered to make appointments to the RTA Board of Directors. The Act did not create a separate body within the Cook County, Illinois, Board of Commissioners. Therefore, the manner of selecting defendants' boards by appointments (rather than election) did not violate the Ill. Const. art. I, § 2 and Ill. Const. art. III, § 3 "one person, one vote" principle. The boards did not have to be constituted so that a majority of its members represented the more populous regions comprising a district. The Act's appointment provisions did not violate equal protection under the "rational basis" test. The Act did not give or deprive the President of the Cook County, Illinois, Board of any inherent powers to appointment to the RTA Board and did not violate Ill. Const. art. II, § 1 (1970) separation of powers or home rule. The legislature was acting within its authority to establish the RTA and provide for its governance.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the appeals court's judgment and affirmed the circuit court's judgment granting defendants' motion to dismiss plaintiffs' complaint.

People v. Boclair


Docket Nos. 89388, 89471, 89534 cons.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


202 Ill. 2d 89; 789 N.E.2d 734; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 371; 273 Ill. Dec. 560



August 29, 2002, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Remanded by People v. Boclair, 342 Ill. App. 3d 1121, 2003 Ill. App. LEXIS 6565 (Ill. App. Ct. 4th Dist., 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
People v. Boclair, 312 Ill. App. 3d 346, 726 N.E.2d 1166, 2000 Ill. App. LEXIS 170, 244 Ill. Dec. 855 (Ill. App. Ct. 4th Dist., 2000)
People v. McCain, 312 Ill. App. 3d 529, 727 N.E.2d 383, 2000 Ill. App. LEXIS 177, 245 Ill. Dec. 130 (Ill. App. Ct. 5th Dist., 2000)
People v. Johnson, 312 Ill. App. 3d 532, 727 N.E.2d 1058, 2000 Ill. App. LEXIS 249, 245 Ill. Dec. 324 (Ill. App. Ct. 5th Dist., 2000)



DISPOSITION: Court of appeals' judgment in Boclair was reversed and case was remanded. Court of appeals' judgments in McCain and Johnson were affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants in these three consolidated cases were convicted of unrelated crimes. Their convictions were affirmed on direct review. Defendants filed petitions for post-conviction relief. The trial court summarily dismissed each petition, and defendants appealed. The Fourth District (Illinois) affirmed one dismissal, and the Fifth District (Illinois) reversed the other dismissals. The state supreme court granted leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendants argued that the trial courts erred in summarily dismissing their post-conviction petitions as untimely during stage one of the post-conviction proceeding process and other errors. The appellate court held that the Illinois Post-Conviction Hearing Act did not authorize the dismissal of a post-conviction petition during the initial stage based on untimeliness. Under a plain reading of 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 122-2.1(a)(2) (West 2000), the circuit court could dismiss a post-conviction petition at the initial stage only if the petition was deemed to be frivolous or patently without merit. Further, time was not an inherent element of the right to bring a post-conviction petition. For that reason, the appellate court held that time limitations in the Post-Conviction Hearing Act should be considered as an affirmative defense and could be raised, waived, or forfeited by the State. Further, a defendant's claim of actual innocence could not be reviewed when a circuit court entered a summary dismissal sua sponte. The state supreme court held the statute was not void for vagueness, and that an amendment to the Act did not violate Ill. Const. art. IV, § 8(d) (1970).


OUTCOME: The judgment, which affirmed the denial of one of defendant's petitions, was reversed and remanded, and the other judgments were affirmed.

People v. Cooper (In re Tekela)


Docket No. 91577


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


202 Ill. 2d 282; 780 N.E.2d 304; 2002 Ill. LEXIS 373; 269 Ill. Dec. 119



August 29, 2002, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 18, 2002.
Rehearing denied by, 12/02/2002
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Cooper v. Illinois, 155 L. Ed. 2d 241, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 2115, 123 S. Ct. 1502 (U.S., Mar. 10, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  People v. Cooper (In re T.J.), 319 Ill. App. 3d 661, 745 N.E.2d 608, 2001 Ill. App. LEXIS 66, 253 Ill. Dec. 485 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Court of appeals' judgment was reversed, in part, vacated, in part, and trial court's order was reinstated in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court issued a summary judgment order terminating appellee mother's parental rights over her three children. The Illinois appellate court reversed and remanded the case. Appellant public guardian informed the appellate court that two of the children had been adopted and filed a motion to vacate the opinion as moot. The appellate court denied the motion, and the guardian and appellant State of Illinois sought review.


OVERVIEW: The trial court terminated the mother's and father's parental rights and appointed a guardian with the power to consent to adoption. After the mother filed a notice of appeal, but before the intermediate appellate court ruled, the children were adopted. The intermediate appellate court, unaware of the adoption, reversed on the basis that unresolved fact questions made summary judgment improper. The mother had not requested a stay to prevent enforcement of the termination order, but argued that a stay was unnecessary because she had filed a notice of appeal. The supreme court disagreed, ruling that the filing of a notice of appeal did not serve as a stay of the termination order. The mother's failure to stay the termination order allowed the adoption to proceed legally. The 12-month time limitation to challenge the adoptions (found at 750 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 50/20b (West 1998)) passed and the adoptions were never challenged. Therefore, the termination issue was moot as to the mother's parental rights over these children. Once it learned of the adoption, the intermediate appellate court should have dismissed the appeal as moot.


OUTCOME: The intermediate appellate court's judgment and remand order as to the two adopted children was reversed. The trial court's order terminating the mother's parental rights over these two children was reinstated. The intermediate appellate court's opinion and remand order as it pertained to the other child had not been appealed from and was not disturbed.
